### Question 95: Can you give me the information about student fees and charges policy?  
### Answer: 

The University acknowledges that sometimes a student will experience circumstances that will impact their ability to fulfill the expectations of the University, including but not limited to assessment, attendance, and paying fees. Circumstances that may fall into this category are: • Exceptional medical circumstances, e.g., hospitalization or incapacitation through injury, illness, or mental health crisis. • Close bereavement, e.g., partner, child, parent, sibling, grandparent, and grandchild. Housemates or very close friends may also be considered as ‘close’, though evidence of the relationship may be required. • Victim of a serious crime • Disabilities for which reasonable adjustments are not yet in place and where the delay is not due to the student • Exceptional and unforeseeable transport difficulties e.g., major transport incidents, cancelled flights. This does not include everyday issues, e.g., traffic congestion, missed buses or trains. • Legal proceedings requiring absence from University. In the case of approved exceptional circumstances, tuition credit will be considered and follow the “Fees & Charges Guide” for undergraduate students. No reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. Students should be aware that course withdrawals may affect their financial aid eligibility if resulting in fewer than the minimum credits for full-time enrollment.

### Question 96: I've lost my student Id card which department should I contact to get back my card?  
### Answer: 

The student should contact the Office of the Registrar. The Office of the Registrar is responsible for maintaining student records and issuing student ID cards. If a student loses their ID card, they should report it to the Office of the Registrar to have it replaced. The student should bring identification to verify their identity and pay any associated fees for a replacement card. The Registrar's Office will then issue a new ID card to the student.

### Question 97: I'm having some bad experience with a professor teaching a course. The problem was his way of delivering does not meet academic standard and I want to say something about this with people having higher authorities. What should I do?  
### Answer: 

If you're experiencing issues with a professor's teaching methods that you believe do not meet academic standards, the first step is to address the issue directly with the professor. This can often resolve the situation. If the problem persists or you feel uncomfortable discussing it with the professor, you should follow the institutional procedures for addressing academic concerns:

1. **Document Everything**: Keep a detailed record of the incidents, including dates, descriptions, and any relevant communications.

2. **Schedule a Meeting**: Request a meeting with the professor to discuss your concerns. Be specific about the issues and provide examples from your experiences.

3. **Consult with Academic Advisor**: If the issue is not resolved, speak with your academic advisor. Advisors are often familiar with faculty members and can offer guidance on how to proceed.

4. **File a Formal Complaint**: If the situation does not improve, you may need to file a formal complaint with the department chair, the dean of the college, or the office of academic affairs. Most universities have a process for handling student complaints about faculty members.

5. **Use the Review of Final Grade Request Form**: If the issue is related to grading, use the appropriate form to request a review of your grade, as outlined in the provided context.

6. **Attend Academic Support Services**: Some universities offer academic support services that can help you navigate these issues. They may provide mediation or additional resources.

7. **Follow the University's Procedures**: Each university has its own set of procedures for addressing academic concerns. Make sure to follow these procedures carefully.

Remember, it's important to approach the situation professionally and respectfully, focusing on the academic issues rather than personal grievances. Also, be aware that while universities aim to support students, they also have to balance the rights of faculty members. The goal is to find a resolution that respects both parties and upholds academic standards.

In summary, start by addressing the issue directly with the professor, then escalate through appropriate channels if necessary, always following the university's established procedures for academic concerns.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]
APPEALS: A student who wishes to dispute a final grade in a course should first discuss the matter with the course instructor(s). If the matter remains unresolved and the student believes there has been an error in grading, bias, or failure to follow published grading policies, the student may file an appeal as follows: • Submit the Review of Final Grade Request form to the Registrar's Office within five (05) business days from the date on which the Registrar's Office posts the final grade for the term. • The Registrar's Office informs the Dean’s Office of the case. After investigation, if the Deans can mediate, no further action is required. Otherwise, the Registrar's Office organizes a panel review with the Dean’s Office for an independent evaluation of the grade. The grade determined after this evaluation shall be the final grade. In cases concerning the interpretation of a course policy, the Dean’s Office will defer to the course syllabus whenever possible and otherwise will make a final ruling on the

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 11]
as specified. 6.2 Academic Warning This is usually the first level of intervention for students who are not making satisfactory progress toward their degree and/or who fall below a 2.0 GPA, or for other reasons such as failure of attendance, participation, citizenship, or academic integrity. An Academic Warning is sent out by the Registrar’s Office. The student is required to attend an Academic Progress Meeting during which a Learning Contract will be created with specific conditions tailored to the individual situation. The Registrar's Office will be informed as a matter of record and follow-up. Three official warnings shall result in Disciplinary Action, constituting Academic Probation or Academic Suspension. 6.3 Academic Probation This is the second level of intervention. It is intended to make students aware that their academic performance puts them at risk of losing the opportunity to study at Fulbright, either temporarily (Suspension) or permanently (Dismissal). It is intended to make students aware that their academic performance puts them at risk of losing the opportunity to study at Fulbright, either temporarily (Suspension) or permanently (Dismissal). Students on Academic Warning who fail to meet all the requirements of their Learning Contract may require an additional Academic Progress Meeting and may be placed on Academic Probation, typically resulting in an updated Learning Contract. Academic Probation typically lasts for a term. 6.4 Academic Suspension A student on Academic Probation who fails to fulfill all the requirements of their Learning Contract may be placed on Academic Suspension. Suspensions are entered on the student’s transcript as part of their academic record. The suspended

### Question 98: I'm a third year student and don't know if I should do a capstone project then where should I go to get advice?  
### Answer: 

You should go to the History department at your university to get advice on the Capstone project. The History Capstone Handbook, AY2025-26, which should be available at your institution, provides detailed guidelines and information on the process. You should also consider discussing your interests and potential project ideas with the faculty members who teach the research seminars you have taken, as they can offer valuable advice and potentially serve as primary advisors for your Capstone project. Additionally, you can reach out to the department's administrative staff or the Capstone coordinator for guidance on the application process and to express your concerns about whether the Capstone is the right choice for you.

The Capstone project is a significant commitment and is not suitable for everyone. It is designed for students who enjoy historical research and want to undertake a long-term research project. If you are unsure whether this is the right path for you, consider your interests, your post-graduation plans, and whether you would prefer to focus on GPA honors instead.

Here are some steps you can take:

1. **Read the Capstone Handbook**: This will give you a comprehensive understanding of what is expected from a Capstone project and the process involved.

2. **Talk to Faculty**: Schedule meetings with faculty members who have expertise in areas that interest you. They can provide guidance on potential projects and the expectations for a Capstone.

3. **Consider Your Interests**: Reflect on what you enjoy most about your studies and whether a Capstone project aligns with those interests.

4. **Discuss with Peers**: Talk to other students who have completed the Capstone project to get their perspectives on the experience.

5. **Seek Guidance from the Department**: Reach out to the Capstone coordinator or the department's administrative staff for personalized advice.

Remember, while the Capstone project can be a rewarding experience, it is not the only path to a successful academic career or to graduating with distinction. It's important to choose a path that aligns with your personal and professional goals.

The Capstone project is a year-long undertaking with less structure than a traditional course. It is certainly not the easiest way to complete your degree, and it may not be the most logical option for you, depending on your interests and your post-graduation plans. You should not consider it to be the default pathway to finishing your degree. One of the best reasons to undertake a Capstone project is if you enjoy doing historical research (which you will have sampled in Hands-On History and the research seminars you have completed) and want an opportunity to carry out a long-term research project. For such students, the Capstone year provides time and guidance that is unparalleled by any course or other opportunity at the undergraduate level. ### Question 99: I'm a member of Fulbright Basketball Club and going to attend a tournament. I need the official transcript and asking for some absence in classes then where should I get help?  
### Answer: 

If you are a member of the Fulbright Basketball Club and need to attend a tournament, you should follow these steps to manage your absence and obtain the necessary documentation:

1. **Contact Your Instructor(s):** As soon as you know about the tournament, you should discuss your planned absence with your course instructor(s). This should ideally be done at least 24 hours prior to the class time you will miss. Remember, simply announcing your absence does not constitute permission for an absence.

2. **University Official Activity Excused Absence Request Form:** Since your absence is for a university-sponsored event, you will need to have a University Official Activity Excused Absence Request Form filled out by the faculty or staff responsible for the basketball club. This form should be submitted to the Dean’s Office, with copies to the Academic Affairs and Registrar’s Office.

3. **Documentation Preparation:** Prepare the necessary documentation to support your request for an excused absence:
   - **Summary of the Tournament:** Write a summary of the program or activity you will be attending (100-200 words).
   - **Proof of Attendance:** Provide proof of your attendance at the tournament, such as an acceptance email, invitation letter, or offer.
   - **Proof of Travel:** Include proof of your travel arrangements, such as flight and accommodation information or an itinerary.

4. **Reflection Paper:** After the tournament, write a 300-500 words reflection paper about your experience and submit it to ug.academic.affairs@fulbright.edu.vn no later than 7 days after you return.

5. **Office of the Registrar:** Upon your return, it is your responsibility to inform the Office of the Registrar about your absence and any arrangements made to make up missed work.

6. **One-Stop Platform:** Ensure you register for classes using the One-Stop platform for the term you return to, if applicable.

By following these steps, you will ensure that your absence is properly documented and approved, and you will be able to make up any missed work. Remember, it is your responsibility to stay on top of your academic responsibilities while participating in university-sponsored activities.

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 7]
STUDENT ENROLLMENT: 3.1 Course Load and Credit Hours Fulbright University Vietnam uses the “credit hour” as the quantum for course workload in the undergraduate program. A credit hour typically represents 3 hours of work during each week of a 16-week term. For a 4- credit hour course, a student would, therefore, be expected to put in 12 hours of work per week, including class time, for a total of 192 hours of work during a term. There are earned credits and attempted credits. Earned credits refer to the number of credit hours a student successfully completes with a grade of D or higher for letter grade options and a Pass for Pass/No Pass options. Attempted credits are the credit hours students enroll in and are recorded in the transcript, regardless of whether they complete the courses or pass them. This includes all courses for which a student receives a grade, including withdrawals (W), incompletes (I), repeats (RP), and failures (F). See section 4 for more details on different types of grades. To graduate  

</details> 

### Question 101: How can I borrow books from the library?  
### Answer: 

You can borrow books from the library by following these steps:

1. Visit the library: Go to the library either in person or through the library's website.

2. Access the library catalog: Use the library's online catalog to search for the books you need. You can also ask the library staff for assistance.

3. Locate the books: Once you've found the books you want, locate them on the shelves. If they're not available, check the library's online system to see if they're on loan or if there are any copies available.

4. Borrow the books: Present your student ID or library card at the circulation desk to borrow the books. The library staff will check the books out to you and note the due date.

5. Return the books: Make sure to return the books on time to avoid late fees. You can return them to the library's return bins or drop boxes if the library is closed.

6. Renew books (if possible): If you need more time with the books, you can usually renew them online or by contacting the library staff.

Remember to familiarize yourself with the library's borrowing policies, including loan periods, renewal limits, and any fines for late returns. Some libraries may also offer interlibrary loan services to borrow books from other institutions if the book you need is not available in your library's collection. Always check with your specific library for their specific procedures and policies.

### Question 102: How to work with major report?  
### Answer: 

To work with the major report for the Capstone by Applied and Creative Project, follow these steps:

1. **Define Clear Objectives**: Clearly articulate the goals and objectives of your project. This will serve as the foundation for your report.

2. **Conduct a Thorough Literature Review**: Understand the context and prior work related to your project by conducting a comprehensive literature review. This will help you position your project within the existing body of knowledge.

3. **Develop a Detailed Project Plan and Timeline**: Outline a detailed plan and timeline for both Capstone 1 and Capstone 2. This plan should include your approach, methods, and activities for each phase of the project.

4. **Prepare the Evaluation Report**: The Evaluation Report is the primary deliverable and should include:
   - **Intellectual Rationale**: Explain the intellectual basis of your project and its expected contributions to social studies.
   - **Goals of the Project**: Clearly state the objectives you aim to achieve.
   - **Evaluation Questions**: Define questions that assess the degree to which your project met its goals.
   - **Critical Evaluation**: Provide a critical evaluation answering the evaluation questions based on the documented process, data collected, and relevant theories from social studies.
   - **Documentation of the Process**: Include relevant research, material experimentation, timelines, and visual records of the project's development stages.

5. **Adhere to Submission Requirements**: Ensure your report meets all submission requirements, including deadline, structure, and word count.

6. **Engage with Advisors**: Work closely with your primary and secondary academic advisors from the SOCI major. For applied and creative projects, you may also work with a non-academic advisor, subject to advisor approval.

7. **Use Appropriate Academic Style and Mechanics**: Format your report according to academic standards, including cover page, illustrations, captions, page numbers, font, margins, etc.

8. **Follow Evaluation Criteria**: Be aware of the evaluation criteria, which focus on the completeness, critical depth, intellectual rigor, and effective use of evidence and theory in your report.

9. **Incorporate Production Documents**: For film or media production projects, include all necessary production documents such as legal release forms, call sheets, test audience feedback, marketing, and distribution plans.

10. **Seek Feedback**: Throughout the process, seek feedback from advisors and peers to refine your report and project.

By following these steps, you will be well-prepared to work effectively with the major report for your Capstone by Applied and Creative Project. Remember, the emphasis is on demonstrating the applicability and social impacts of your project, supported by rigorous evaluation and evidence.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: SOCIAL STUDIES CAPSTONE HANDBOOK 2025-2026.pdf, Page: 23]

### Question 103: How many way can I fill in the requirements of EL?  
### Answer: 

There are two ways to fulfill the requirements of EL: by completing at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement, or by having experiential learning count toward elective or major requirements (upon advisor approval).

<summary>References</summary>

count toward elective or major requirements (upon advisor approval). Fulbright’s Experiential Learning Program (ELP) exists to create, standardize, and facilitate academic credit-bearing experiential learning experiences. All full-time, undergraduate, degree- seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement. Current ELP streams are housed within Academic Affairs (AA), Career Services (CS), and Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation (CEI), where each team is responsible for administrative and academic duties of their respective stream(s). The ELP operates within Fulbright’s undergraduate program’s standard operating procedures and is developed in consultation with undergraduate program leadership and faculty. For more details, please refer here https://onestop.fulbright.edu.vn/s/article/Experiential-Learning- Program 13.5  Electives To complete the degree, students are free to take elective courses from the Course Catalogue. While the number of elective credits may vary, the total number of earned credits must be at least 128. Electives enable students to explore different subjects across the curriculum to build a broad and diverse background or to pursue more specialized studies within their major. Alternatively, students may use electives to fulfill requirements for another minor or major. 13.6  Capstone All majors at Fulbright offer an optional capstone pathway to be completed during the student’s final year. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. Fulbright’s Experiential Learning Program (ELP) exists to create, standardize, and facilitate academic credit-bearing experiential learning experiences. All full-time, undergraduate, degree- seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement. [Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
number of elective credits may vary, the total number of earned credits must be at least 128. Electives enable students to explore different subjects across the curriculum to build a broad and diverse background or to pursue more specialized studies within their major. Alternatively, students may use electives to fulfill requirements for another minor or major. 13.6  Capstone All majors at Fulbright offer an optional capstone pathway to be completed during the student’s final year. Students may apply for the opportunity to fulfill 8 credits of their major requirements via a capstone project. A student wishing to do a capstone must apply during the term prior to the first term of the capstone (Capstone I), and may only apply after having completed 80 credits of coursework. Students who do not do a capstone will need to fulfill 8 credits of coursework as specified in their major.
